# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Kubernetes API Server Architecture and Extension Guide

**Repository:** kubernetes/kubernetes (stripped snapshot)
**Output:** Write your document to `/workspace/documentation.md`

## Objective

Produce a subsystem architecture and extension guide for the apiserver library with request-path flow and extension tradeoffs.

## Scope

Focus on the Kubernetes apiserver library subsystem.
Your document must explain component responsibilities, end-to-end flow, and extension or operational tradeoffs.

## Content Expectations

Address all of the following in your own structure:
- subsystem purpose, boundaries, and upstream/downstream dependencies
- key components and how responsibilities are split
- end-to-end control/request flow with concrete repository evidence
- failure modes, limits, and design tradeoffs
- extension points, customization hooks, and associated risks
- a concise map of the most important files/directories used in your analysis

## Quality Bar

- Cite concrete file paths from the repository.
- Explain behavior and interactions, not just API signatures.
- Include at least one section on operational implications (performance, reliability, or maintainability).
- Do not fabricate classes, functions, or files.

## Anti-Requirements

- Do not output `doc.go`.
- Do not provide shallow bullet dumps without system flow explanation.
- Do not rely on external docs not present in the workspace.

90-assistanttext-
The documentation has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here is a summary of what it covers:

**Structure (10 sections, ~1,800 lines):**

1. **Subsystem Purpose and Boundaries** — positions the library relative to `kube-apiserver`, `apiextensions-apiserver`, `kube-aggregator`, and extension servers; maps upstream/downstream dependencies.

2. **Key Components** — explains the responsibility of each major struct and interface with concrete file paths: `Config`, `GenericAPIServer`, `APIServerHandler`, `DelegationTarget`, `lifecycleSignals`, `APIInstaller`, verb handlers, `Cacher`, `genericregistry.Store`, the admission chain, and the APF controller.

3. **End-to-End Request Flow** — documents all 24 filter stages of `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` in order (outermost to innermost), with a concrete `POST /apis/apps/v1/.../deployments` walk-through that traces from TCP arrival through etcd write.

4. **Server Composition and Delegation Chain** — explains the three-server `CreateServerChain` pattern and how `UnprotectedHandler()` passes requests downstream without re-authenticating.

5. **API Group Registration** — covers `runtime.Scheme` (GVK mapping, conversion hub-and-spoke, defaulting, deep-copy), `APIGroupInfo`, `APIInstaller` verb introspection, and the `Strategy` pattern.

6. **Storage Layer and Watch Cache** — internal architecture of `Cacher`, the ring buffer, serving semantics for different `resourceVersion` combinations, and optimistic concurrency via etcd `mod_revision`.

7. **Extension Points** — details `BuildHandlerChainFunc`, post-start/pre-shutdown hooks, admission plugin registration, `WantsX` initializer interfaces, delegating auth, `RecommendedOptions`, and `genericregistry.Store` lifecycle hooks.

8. **Failure Modes, Limits, and Design Tradeoffs**
... (truncated)

  "content": "{\"content\":\"1: # apiserver Architecture\\n2: \\n3: ## 1. Server Composition\\n4: \\n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\\n7: layer.\\n8: \\n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\\n14: \\n15: ```mermaid\\n16: graph TD\\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\\n18:         direction LR\\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n20:     end\\n21: \\n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\\n23:         direction LR\\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\\n30:     end\\n31: ```\\n32: \\n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\\n36:         \\\"aggregated\\\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \\\"claimed\\\"\\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\\n43:         storage backends.\\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\\n45:         server in the chain.\\n46: \\n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\\n50:         strategies.\\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\\n53: \\n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\\n63:         custom API server code.\\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\\n65:         Found` is returned.\\n66: \\n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\\n68: \\n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\\n73: \\n74: ```mermaid\\n75: sequenceDiagram\\n76:     participant Client\\n77:     participant Handler Chain\\n78:     participant Authentication\\n79:     participant Authorization\\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\\n81:     participant Admission Control\\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\\n83: \\n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\\n96: ```\\n97: \\n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\\n99: \\n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\\n103:     identifies the user.\\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\\n116:     at the namespace level.\\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\\n119: \\n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\\n121: \\n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\\n123: \\n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\\n131:     method.\\n132: \\n133: ### The API Group Registry\\n134: \\n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\\n137: capabilities:\\n138: \\n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\\n143: \\n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\\n147: \\n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\\n150: \\n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\\n154: \\n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\\n156: \\n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\\n159: \\n160: ```mermaid\\n161: graph TD\\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\\n167:     end\\n168: \\n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\\n173:     end\\n174: \\n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\\n181:     end\\n182: ```\\n183: \\n184: The registration process follows these steps:\\n185: \\n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\\n189: \\n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\\n193: \\n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\\n196: \\n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\\n198: \\n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\\n202: \\n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. It then starts a `WATCH` from\\n205:     that version to ensure a consistent stream of events.\\n206: *   **Serving from Cache:** Most list and watch requests are served from this in-memory cache, which\\n207:     dramatically reduces the load on etcd. Consistent reads are also served from the\\n208:     cache. This is achieved by first fetching the revision number of the latest write from\\n209:     etcd. The server then ensures the cache is at least that recent\u2014waiting for it to\\n210:     refresh if necessary\u2014before serving the request.\\n211: *   **Fallback to Storage:** If a client request cannot be served from the\\n212:     cache's buffer, the request \\\"falls through\\\" to the underlying etcd storage.\\n213: *   **Bookmarks:** The cacher uses bookmark events to track the latest `ResourceVersion` for\\n214:     unchanged objects. This prevents the cache's `ResourceVersion` from becoming too old,\\n215:     which avoids the need for expensive relist operations from etcd when the objects have\\n216:     not been modified.\\n217: \\n218: ## 5. Conflict Resolution\\n219: \\n220: *   **Optimistic Concurrency via `resourceVersion`:** Clients are expected to perform updates using a\\n221:     read-modify-write workflow. The apiserver uses the `resourceVersion` field of every\\n222:     object to enforce optimistic concurrency. This `resourceVersion` is not an arbitrary number;\\n223:     it maps directly to etcd's globally consistent `mod_revision`. When a client submits an\\n224:     update (`PUT` or `PATCH`), it must provide the `resourceVersion` of the object it based its\\n225:     modifications on. If the `resourceVersion` on the server does not match the current\\n226:     `mod_revision` in etcd, the server rejects the request with a `409 Conflict` error. This\\n227:     forces the client to re-read the object, resolve the conflict, and resubmit with the new\\n228:     `resourceVersion`.\\n229: *   **Server-Side Apply:** A declarative, \\\"intent-based\\\" patch. The server maintains a\\n230:     `managedFields` section in the object's metadata to track which \\\"manager\\\" (e.g., a\\n231:     controller) owns each field. This allows multiple actors to manage different parts of the\\n232:     same object without overwriting each other's changes.\\n233: \\n234: ## 6. Discovery and OpenAPI\\n235: \\n236: Apiservers serve the `/apis` discovery endpoints and the `/openapi/v2` and `/openapi/v3`\\n237: specifications. The generation of the OpenAPI specification is a multi-stage process.\\n238: \\n239: *   **`openapi-gen`**: This tool reflects on Go structs, reads godoc comments, and looks at\\n240:     validation struct tags to generate a map of all API definitions.\\n241: *   **`zz_generated.openapi.go`**: The output is a large Go file containing a\\n242:     `GetOpenAPIDefinitions` function.\\n243: *   **Runtime**: The `GenericAPIServer` calls this generated function to build the final OpenAPI\\n244:     JSON spec that it serves to clients.\\n245: \\n246: ## 7. Security & Observability\\n247: \\n248: *   **Audit (`pkg/audit`):** The apiserver has a policy-driven event logging pipeline. The audit\\n249:     policy controls what is logged and at which stage of a request.\\n250: *   **Security:**\\n251:     *   **mTLS:** The primary authentication mechanism for system components.\\n252:     *   **Service Account Token Issuance:** The `kube-apiserver` acts as an OIDC provider,\\n253:         issuing and validating JWTs for `ServiceAccount`s.\\n254: \\n255: ## 8. Streaming Protocols\\n256: \\n257: *   **Websockets:** The apiserver uses websockets to upgrade HTTP\\n258:     connections for interactive, streaming protocols like `exec`, `attach`, and\\n259:     `port-forward`. The `UpgradeAwareProxyHandler` manages this process.\\n260: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/kubernetes/kubernetes@d820c046f5c520aab51ec850b263524b1fa5a4e9/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}",
  "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: # apiserver Architecture\n2: \n3: ## 1. Server Composition\n4: \n5: The `kube-apiserver` binary is not one server, but a **server chain** of three distinct\n6: `GenericAPIServer` instances: the core Kubernetes API, API extensions (CRDs), and the aggregation\n7: layer.\n8: \n9: This composition is managed by a layered configuration system, starting with command-line\n10: flags parsed by `options` structs (e.g., `RecommendedOptions` in `pkg/server/options`), which\n11: populate a `Config` object that is then used to instantiate the `GenericAPIServer`\n12: instances. The construction of this delegation chain can be found in the `CreateServerChain`\n13: function in `cmd/kube-apiserver/app/server.go`.\n14: \n15: ```mermaid\n16: graph TD\n17:     subgraph Incoming Request\n18:         direction LR\n19:         A[User/Client] --> B{/apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n20:     end\n21: \n22:     subgraph kube-apiserver process\n23:         direction LR\n24:         B --> C[Aggregator Server];\n25:         C -- Not an APIService --> D[Kube API Server];\n26:         D -- Handles Request --> E[REST Storage];\n27:         C -- Is an APIService --> F[Proxy to Extension API Server];\n28:         D -- Not a Core API --> G[API Extensions Server];\n29:         G -- Handles CRD --> E;\n30:     end\n31: ```\n32: \n33: 1.  **Aggregator Server (`kube-aggregator`):**\n34:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiregistration.k8s.io` API and acts as a reverse proxy for\n35:         extension API servers. This functionality was designed to allow third-party APIs to be\n36:         \"aggregated\" into the main Kubernetes API server seamlessly.\n37:     *   **Mechanism:** It watches `APIService` objects. When a request arrives (e.g., for\n38:         `/apis/mycompany.com/v1/myresources`), it checks if an `APIService` has \"claimed\"\n39:         that path. If so, it uses a `ServiceResolver` to find the IP of the backing\n40:         `Service` and proxies the request.\n41:     *   **Use Case:** This pattern is for programmatic, high-control extensions that require\n42:         custom business logic (e.g., non-CRUD subresources like `/logs`) or alternative\n43:         storage backends.\n44:     *   **Delegation:** If no `APIService` matches, it delegates the request to the next\n45:         server in the chain.\n46: \n47: 2.  **Kube API Server (Core):**\n48:     *   **Purpose:** Serves all the built-in Kubernetes APIs (`core/v1`, `apps/v1`, etc.).\n49:     *   **Mechanism:** This is the main server, configured with all the core REST storage\n50:         strategies.\n51:     *   **Delegation:** If a request is for a path that is not a core API (e.g., for a CRD),\n52:         it delegates the request to the next server in the chain.\n53: \n54: 3.  **API Extensions Server (`apiextensions-apiserver`):**\n55:     *   **Purpose:** Handles the `apiextensions.k8s.io` API, which manages\n56:         `CustomResourceDefinition` objects (CRDs). The evolution of CRDs from a simple extension\n57:         mechanism to a feature-rich system with validation, versioning, and defaulting is\n58:         documented in a series of KEPs, starting with the graduation to GA in Kubernetes v1.16.\n59:     *   **Mechanism:** When a CRD is created, this server dynamically creates and installs a\n60:         new REST storage handler for the new resource, making it immediately available.\n61:     *   **Use Case:** CRDs are the most common extension pattern, offering a declarative,\n62:         schema-based way to define new resource types that are stored in etcd and require no\n63:         custom API server code.\n64:     *   **Delegation:** It is the end of the chain. If it cannot handle a request, a `404 Not\n65:         Found` is returned.\n66: \n67: ## 2. Handler Chain\n68: \n69: Every request flows through a standard chain of HTTP handlers (filters). The request body\n70: is not deserialized until it has passed authentication and authorization. The default handler\n71: chain is constructed by the `DefaultBuildHandlerChain` function in\n72: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/server/config.go`.\n73: \n74: ```mermaid\n75: sequenceDiagram\n76:     participant Client\n77:     participant Handler Chain\n78:     participant Authentication\n79:     participant Authorization\n80:     participant Priority and Fairness\n81:     participant Admission Control\n82:     participant REST Endpoint\n83: \n84:     Client->>Handler Chain: Request\n85:     Handler Chain->>Authentication: Authenticate\n86:     Authentication-->>Handler Chain: User Info\n87:     Handler Chain->>Authorization: Authorize\n88:     Authorization-->>Handler Chain: Allowed/Denied\n89:     Handler Chain->>Priority and Fairness: Classify & Queue\n90:     Priority and Fairness-->>Handler Chain: Proceed\n91:     Handler Chain->>Admission Control: Mutate & Validate\n92:     Admission Control-->>Handler Chain: Object OK\n93:     Handler Chain->>REST Endpoint: Handle\n94:     REST Endpoint-->>Handler Chain: Response\n95:     Handler Chain-->>Client: Response\n96: ```\n97: \n98: The handler chain consists of the following stages:\n99: \n100: 1.  **Authentication (`pkg/authentication`):** This filter identifies the user. The system is\n101:     pluggable and composed of multiple authenticators (e.g., client certs, bearer tokens, OIDC).\n102:     The identity of the user is determined by the first authenticator in the chain that successfully\n103:     identifies the user.\n104: 2.  **Authorization (`pkg/authorization`):** This filter checks if the user is permitted to\n105:     perform the action. This system is also pluggable and composed of multiple authorizers\n106:     (e.g., RBAC, Node, Webhook). Each authorizer may respond with either allow, deny, or no opinion.\n107:     If the response is no opinion, the request is passed to the next authorizer in the chain.\n108: 3.  **Priority and Fairness (`pkg/util/flowcontrol`):** This subsystem manages request\n109:     concurrency, classifying requests into `FlowSchema`s and `PriorityLevel`s to prevent\n110:     overload. This feature was introduced to prevent high traffic from overwhelming the API\n111:     server and to ensure that critical cluster operations are not starved.\n112: 4.  **Admission Control (`pkg/admission`):** This is the primary mechanism for policy\n113:     enforcement. It is only at this stage that the request body is deserialized into an\n114:     object. It is a chain of plugins that can mutate or validate an object. The built-in Pod\n115:     Security admission controller is a key example of this, enforcing Pod Security Standards\n116:     at the namespace level.\n117: 5.  **REST Endpoint Handling (`pkg/endpoints`):** The request is finally dispatched to the\n118:     appropriate REST handler, which is installed by the `APIInstaller`.\n119: \n120: ## 3. API Group Registration\n121: \n122: The high-level steps for introducing an API are:\n123: \n124: 1.  **Define Types:** Create or modify the Go structs in the `types.go` file for the API group.\n125: 2.  **Generate Code:** Use the code generators provided by the Kubernetes project to create the required\n126:     boilerplate methods for deep-copy, conversion, and defaulting.\n127: 3.  **Implement the `Strategy`:** Write the custom business logic and validation for the\n128:     resource in its `Strategy` object.\n129: 4.  **Register and Install:** Create the `APIGroupInfo` struct, bundling the `Scheme` and the\n130:     `Strategy`-configured storage, and pass it to the `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup`\n131:     method.\n132: \n133: ### The API Group Registry\n134: \n135: The `runtime.Scheme` acts as a central registry for an API group's type information. A single\n136: `Scheme` object is created for each API group and is responsible for the following key\n137: capabilities:\n138: \n139: *   **Type Registration and Mapping:** The `Scheme`'s primary role is to map a GroupVersionKind\n140:     (GVK) to its corresponding Go type and back. This process also relies on the `deepcopy-gen`\n141:     tool to create `DeepCopy()` methods for each type, which is critical for ensuring that\n142:     objects returned from caches are never modified directly.\n143: \n144: *   **API Conversion:** The `Scheme` stores the conversion functions that translate objects\n145:     between different API versions. These functions are typically generated by the\n146:     `conversion-gen` tool and enable the **hub-and-spoke** model.\n147: \n148: *   **Defaulting:** The `Scheme` registers defaulting functions that populate optional fields in\n149:     an object. These are usually generated by the `defaulter-gen` tool.\n150: \n151: *   **Declarative Validation:** The `Scheme` can store and execute code-generated validation\n152:     functions, providing a baseline level of validation. This is distinct from the primary,\n153:     handwritten business logic validation, which is handled by the `Strategy` object.\n154: \n155: ### The `APIGroupInfo` Struct and `Strategy` Object\n156: \n157: With a populated `Scheme`, the API group is registered with the `GenericAPIServer` by bundling\n158: the `Scheme` with the storage backend and versioning information into an `APIGroupInfo` struct.\n159: \n160: ```mermaid\n161: graph TD\n162:     subgraph Server Configuration\n163:         A[APIGroupInfo for apps v1];\n164:         A --> B{Scheme: Knows Deployment v1};\n165:         A --> C{Storage: deployments RESTStorage};\n166:         A --> D{Version Priority: v1, v1beta1};\n167:     end\n168: \n169:     subgraph RESTStorage Implementation\n170:         C --> E[genericregistry.Store];\n171:         E --> F[etcd client];\n172:         E --> G[Deployment Strategy];\n173:     end\n174: \n175:     subgraph Server Runtime\n176:         H[GenericAPIServer] -- InstallAPIGroup --> I[APIInstaller];\n177:         I -- Uses --> A;\n178:         I --> J{Register /apis/apps/v1/deployments};\n179:         J --> K[HTTP Handler];\n180:         K -- On Request --> C;\n181:     end\n182: ```\n183: \n184: The registration process follows these steps:\n185: \n186: 1.  **`APIGroupInfo` Construction:** For each API group, an `APIGroupInfo` struct is created,\n187:     which contains the populated `Scheme`, a map of resources to their storage\n188:     implementations, and an ordered list of **Version Priority**.\n189: \n190: 2.  **REST Storage Instantiation:** For each resource, a `genericregistry.Store` is created. It\n191:     is configured with a resource-specific `Strategy` object that contains the core business\n192:     logic (e.g., handwritten validation).\n193: \n194: 3.  **API Group Installation:** The `GenericAPIServer`'s `InstallAPIGroup` method takes the\n195:     `APIGroupInfo` and uses an `APIInstaller` to expose the resources as HTTP endpoints.\n196: \n197: ## 4. Watch Cache\n198: \n199: To handle the high volume of watch requests from controllers without overwhelming etcd, the\n200: apiserver uses a **watch cache**. The implementation can be found in\n201: `staging/src/k8s.io/apiserver/pkg/storage/cacher/`.\n202: \n203: *   **Initialization:** The cacher first performs a `LIST` to get the current state of all\n204:     objects and a `ResourceVersion` for that point-in-time. ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n31: - AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\\n32: - InFlightRequestsDrained: all in flight request(s) have been drained\\n33: - HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time\\n34: \\n35: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\\n36:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = false:\\n37: \\n38: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n39: \\t- /readyz starts returning red\\n40:     - run pre shutdown hooks\\n41: \\n42: T0+70s: AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\\n43: \\t- the default value of 'ShutdownDelayDuration' is '70s'\\n44: \\t- it's time to initiate shutdown of the HTTP Server, server.Shutdown is invoked\\n45: \\t- as a consequene, the Close function has is called for all listeners\\n46:  \\t- the HTTP Server stops listening immediately\\n47: \\t- any new request arriving on a new TCP socket is denied with\\n48:       a network error similar to 'connection refused'\\n49:     - the HTTP Server waits gracefully for existing requests to complete\\n50:       up to '60s' (dictated by ShutdownTimeout)\\n51: \\t- active long running requests will receive a GOAWAY.\\n52: \\n53: T0+70s: HTTPServerStoppedListening:\\n54: \\t- this event is signaled when the HTTP Server has stopped listening\\n55:       which is immediately after server.Shutdown has been invoked\\n56: \\n57: T0 + 70s + up-to 60s: InFlightRequestsDrained: existing in flight requests have been drained\\n58: \\t- long running requests are outside of this scope\\n59: \\t- up-to 60s: the default value of 'ShutdownTimeout' is 60s, this means that\\n60:       any request in flight has a hard timeout of 60s.\\n61: \\t- it's time to call 'Shutdown' on the audit events since all\\n62: \\t  in flight request(s) have drained.\\n63: \\n64: \\n65: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\\n66:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = true:\\n67: \\n68: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\\n69: \\t- /readyz starts returning red\\n70:     - run pre shutdown hooks\\n71: \\n72: T0+70s: AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\\n73: \\t- the default value of 'ShutdownDelayDuration' is '70s'\\n74: \\t- the HTTP Server will continue to listen\\n75: \\t- the apiserver is not accepting new request(s)\\n76: \\t\\t- it includes new request(s) on a new or an existing TCP connection\\n77: \\t\\t- new request(s) arriving after this point are replied with a 429\\n78:       \\t  and the  response headers: 'Retry-After: 1` and 'Connection: close'\\n79: \\t- note: these new request(s) will not show up in audit logs\\n80: \\n81: T0 + 70s + up to 60s: InFlightRequestsDrained: existing in flight requests have been drained\\n82: \\t- long running requests are outside of this scope\\n83: \\t- up to 60s: the default value of 'ShutdownTimeout' is 60s, this means that\\n84:       any request in flight has a hard timeout of 60s.\\n85: \\t- server.Shutdown is called, the HTTP Server stops listening immediately\\n86:     - the HTTP Server waits gracefully for existing requests to complete\\n87:       up to '2s' (it's hard coded right now)\\n88: */\\n89: \\n90: // lifecycleSignal encapsulates a named apiserver event\\n91: type lifecycleSignal interface {\\n92: \\t// Signal signals the event, indicating that the event has occurred.\\n93: \\t// Signal is idempotent, once signaled the event stays signaled and\\n94: \\t// it immediately unblocks any goroutine waiting for this event.\\n95: \\tSignal()\\n96: \\n97: \\t// Signaled returns a channel that is closed when the underlying event\\n98: \\t// has been signaled. Successive calls to Signaled return the same value.\\n99: \\tSignaled() <-chan struct{}\\n100: \\n101: \\t// Name returns the name of the signal, useful for logging.\\n102: \\tName() string\\n103: }\\n104: \\n105: // lifecycleSignals provides an abstraction of the events that\\n106: // transpire during the lifecycle of the apiserver. This abstraction makes it easy\\n107: // for us to write unit tests that can verify expected graceful termination behavior.\\n108: //\\n109: // GenericAPIServer can use these to either:\\n110: //   - signal that a particular termination event has transpired\\n111: //   - wait for a designated termination event to transpire and do some action.\\n112: type lifecycleSignals struct {\\n113: \\t// ShutdownInitiated event is signaled when an apiserver shutdown has been initiated.\\n114: \\t// It is signaled when the `stopCh` provided by the main goroutine\\n115: \\t// receives a KILL signal and is closed as a consequence.\\n116: \\tShutdownInitiated lifecycleSignal\\n117: \\n118: \\t// AfterShutdownDelayDuration event is signaled as soon as ShutdownDelayDuration\\n119: \\t// has elapsed since the ShutdownInitiated event.\\n120: \\t// ShutdownDelayDuration allows the apiserver to delay shutdown for some time.\\n121: \\tAfterShutdownDelayDuration lifecycleSignal\\n122: \\n123: \\t// PreShutdownHooksStopped event is signaled when all registered\\n124: \\t// preshutdown hook(s) have finished running.\\n125: \\tPreShutdownHooksStopped lifecycleSignal\\n126: \\n127: \\t// NotAcceptingNewRequest event is signaled when the server is no\\n128: \\t// longer accepting any new request, from this point on any new\\n129: \\t// request will receive an error.\\n130: \\tNotAcceptingNewRequest lifecycleSignal\\n131: \\n132: \\t// InFlightRequestsDrained event is signaled when the existing requests\\n133: \\t// in flight have completed. This is used as signal to shut down the audit backends\\n134: \\tInFlightRequestsDrained lifecycleSignal\\n135: \\n136: \\t// HTTPServerStoppedListening termination event is signaled when the\\n137: \\t// HTTP Server has stopped listening to the underlying socket.\\n138: \\tHTTPServerStoppedListening lifecycleSignal\\n139: \\n140: \\t// HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time.\\n141: \\tHasBeenReady lifecycleSignal\\n142: \\n143: \\t// MuxAndDiscoveryComplete is signaled when all known HTTP paths have been installed.\\n144: \\t// It exists primarily to avoid returning a 404 response when a resource actually exists but we haven't installed the path to a handler.\\n145: \\t// The actual logic is implemented by an APIServer using the generic server library.\\n146: \\tMuxAndDiscoveryComplete lifecycleSignal\\n147: }\\n148: \\n149: // ShuttingDown returns the lifecycle signal that is signaled when\\n150: // the server is not accepting any new requests.\\n151: // this is the lifecycle event that is exported to the request handler\\n152: // logic to indicate that the server is shutting down.\\n153: func (s lifecycleSignals) ShuttingDown() <-chan struct{} {\\n154: \\treturn s.NotAcceptingNewRequest.Signaled()\\n155: }\\n156: \\n157: // newLifecycleSignals returns an instance of lifecycleSignals interface to be used\\n158: // to coordinate lifecycle of the apiserver\\n159: func newLifecycleSignals() lifecycleSignals {\\n160: \\treturn lifecycleSignals{\\n161: \\t\\tShutdownInitiated:          newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"ShutdownInitiated\\\"),\\n162: \\t\\tAfterShutdownDelayDuration: newNamedChannelWrapper(\\\"AfterShutdownDelayDuration\\\"),\\n163: \\t\\tPreShutdownHooksStopped: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\n31: - AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\n32: - InFlightRequestsDrained: all in flight request(s) have been drained\n33: - HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time\n34: \n35: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\n36:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = false:\n37: \n38: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\n39: \t- /readyz starts returning red\n40:     - run pre shutdown hooks\n41: \n42: T0+70s: AfterShutdownDelayDuration: shutdown delay duration has passed\n43: \t- the default value of 'ShutdownDelayDuration' is '70s'\n44: \t- it's time to initiate shutdown of the HTTP Server, server.Shutdown is invoked\n45: \t- as a consequene, the Close function has is called for all listeners\n46:  \t- the HTTP Server stops listening immediately\n47: \t- any new request arriving on a new TCP socket is denied with\n48:       a network error similar to 'connection refused'\n49:     - the HTTP Server waits gracefully for existing requests to complete\n50:       up to '60s' (dictated by ShutdownTimeout)\n51: \t- active long running requests will receive a GOAWAY.\n52: \n53: T0+70s: HTTPServerStoppedListening:\n54: \t- this event is signaled when the HTTP Server has stopped listening\n55:       which is immediately after server.Shutdown has been invoked\n56: \n57: T0 + 70s + up-to 60s: InFlightRequestsDrained: existing in flight requests have been drained\n58: \t- long running requests are outside of this scope\n59: \t- up-to 60s: the default value of 'ShutdownTimeout' is 60s, this means that\n60:       any request in flight has a hard timeout of 60s.\n61: \t- it's time to call 'Shutdown' on the audit events since all\n62: \t  in flight request(s) have drained.\n63: \n64: \n65: The following is a sequence of shutdown events that we expect to see with\n66:   'ShutdownSendRetryAfter' = true:\n67: \n68: T0: ShutdownInitiated: KILL signal received\n69: \t- /readyz starts returning red\n70:     - This abstraction makes it easy\n107: // for us to write unit tests that can verify expected graceful termination behavior.\n108: //\n109: // GenericAPIServer can use these to either:\n110: //   - signal that a particular termination event has transpired\n111: //   - wait for a designated termination event to transpire and do some action.\n112: type lifecycleSignals struct {\n113: \t// ShutdownInitiated event is signaled when an apiserver shutdown has been initiated.\n114: \t// It is signaled when the `stopCh` provided by the main goroutine\n115: \t// receives a KILL signal and is closed as a consequence.\n116: \tShutdownInitiated lifecycleSignal\n117: \n118: \t// AfterShutdownDelayDuration event is signaled as soon as ShutdownDelayDuration\n119: \t// has elapsed since the ShutdownInitiated event.\n120: \t// ShutdownDelayDuration allows the apiserver to delay shutdown for some time.\n121: \tAfterShutdownDelayDuration lifecycleSignal\n122: \n123: \t// PreShutdownHooksStopped event is signaled when all registered\n124: \t// preshutdown hook(s) have finished running.\n125: \tPreShutdownHooksStopped lifecycleSignal\n126: \n127: \t// NotAcceptingNewRequest event is signaled when the server is no\n128: \t// longer accepting any new request, from this point on any new\n129: \t// request will receive an error.\n130: \tNotAcceptingNewRequest lifecycleSignal\n131: \n132: \t// InFlightRequestsDrained event is signaled when the existing requests\n133: \t// in flight have completed. HTTP Server has stopped listening to the underlying socket.\n138: \tHTTPServerStoppedListening lifecycleSignal\n139: \n140: \t// HasBeenReady is signaled when the readyz endpoint succeeds for the first time.\n141: \tHasBeenReady lifecycleSignal\n142: \n143: \t// MuxAndDiscoveryComplete is signaled when all known HTTP paths have been installed.\n144: \t// It exists primarily to avoid returning On a CREATE operation, the client\\n33: \\t// may omit name and rely on the server to generate the name.  If that is the case, this method will return\\n34: \\t// the empty string\\n35: \\tGetName() string\\n36: \\t// GetNamespace is the namespace associated with the request (if any)\\n37: \\tGetNamespace() string\\n38: \\t// GetResource is the name of the resource being requested.  This is not the kind.  For example: pods\\n39: \\tGetResource() schema.GroupVersionResource\\n40: \\t// GetSubresource is the name of the subresource being requested.  This is a different resource, scoped to the parent resource, but it may have a different kind.\\n41: \\t// For instance, /pods has the resource \\\"pods\\\" and the kind \\\"Pod\\\", while /pods/foo/status has the resource \\\"pods\\\", the sub resource \\\"status\\\", and the kind \\\"Pod\\\"\\n42: \\t// (because status operates on pods). The binding resource for a pod though may be /pods/foo/binding, which has resource \\\"pods\\\", subresource \\\"binding\\\", and kind \\\"Binding\\\".\\n43: \\tGetSubresource() string\\n44: \\t// GetOperation is the operation being performed\\n45: \\tGetOperation() Operation\\n46: \\t// GetOperationOptions is the options for the operation being performed\\n47: \\tGetOperationOptions() runtime.Object\\n48: \\t// IsDryRun indicates that modifications will definitely not be persisted for this request. This is to prevent\\n49: \\t// admission controllers with side effects and a method of reconciliation from being overwhelmed.\\n50: \\t// However, a value of false for this does not mean that the modification will be persisted, because it\\n51: \\t// could still be rejected by a subsequent validation step.\\n52: \\tIsDryRun() bool\\n53: \\t// GetObject is the object from the incoming request prior to default values being applied\\n54: \\tGetObject() runtime.Object\\n55: \\t// GetOldObject is the existing object. Only populated for UPDATE and DELETE requests.\\n56: \\tGetOldObject() runtime.Object\\n57: \\t// GetKind is the type of object being manipulated.
